<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the chemical element. <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_tin" title="Template:Infobox tin"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_tin" title="Template talk:Infobox tin"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_tin&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div> | <a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Tin</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with the symbol <b>Sn</b> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">stannum</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 50. It is a <a href="/wiki/Post-transition_metal" title="Post-transition metal">post-transition metal</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Group_14" title="Group 14">group 14</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periodic_table_of_elements" title="Periodic table of elements">periodic table of elements</a>. It is obtained chiefly from the <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">mineral</a> <a href="/wiki/Cassiterite" title="Cassiterite">cassiterite</a>, which contains <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tin_dioxide" title="Tin dioxide">stannic oxide</a>, SnO<sub>2</sub>. Tin shows a chemical similarity to both of its neighbors in group 14, <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, and has two main <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation states</a>, +2 and the slightly more stable +4. Tin is the 49th most abundant element and has, with 10 stable isotopes, the largest number of stable <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a> in the periodic table, thanks to its <a href="/wiki/Magic_number_(physics)" title="Magic number (physics)">magic number</a> of protons. It has two main <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allotrope" title="Allotrope">allotropes</a>: at room temperature, the stable allotrope is β-tin, a silvery-white, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malleable" title="Malleable">malleable</a> metal, but at low temperatures it transforms into the less dense grey α-tin, which has the <a href="/wiki/Diamond_cubic" title="Diamond cubic">diamond cubic</a> structure. Metallic tin does not easily <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidation" title="Oxidation">oxidize</a> in air.
</p><p>The first tin <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloy</a> used on a large scale was <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a>, made of tin and <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, from as early as 3000 BC. After 600 BC, pure metallic tin was produced. <a href="/wiki/Pewter" title="Pewter">Pewter</a>, which is an alloy of 85–90% tin with the remainder commonly consisting of <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a>, and lead, was used for <a href="/wiki/Tableware" title="Tableware">flatware</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> until the 20th century. In modern times, tin is used in many alloys, most notably tin/lead soft <a href="/wiki/Solder" title="Solder">solders</a>, which are typically 60% or more tin and in the manufacture of transparent, electrically conducting films of <a href="/wiki/Indium_tin_oxide" title="Indium tin oxide">indium tin oxide</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optoelectronic" title="Optoelectronic">optoelectronic</a> applications. Another large application for tin is <a href="/wiki/Corrosion" title="Corrosion">corrosion</a>-resistant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tin_plating" title="Tin plating">tin plating</a> of <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a>. Because of the low toxicity of inorganic tin, tin-plated steel is widely used for food packaging as <a href="/wiki/Tin_can" title="Tin can">tin cans</a>. However, some <a href="/wiki/Organotin_chemistry" title="Organotin chemistry">organotin compounds</a> can be almost as toxic as <a href="/wiki/Cyanide" title="Cyanide">cyanide</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical">Physical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Physical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tin-2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1144" data-file-width="1144" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Tin-2.jpg/220px-Tin-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Tin-2.jpg/330px-Tin-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Tin-2.jpg/440px-Tin-2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tin-2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Drop_(liquid)" title="Drop (liquid)">Droplet</a> of solidified molten tin</div></div></div>
<p>Tin is a soft, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malleable" title="Malleable">malleable</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ductile" title="Ductile">ductile</a> and highly <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">crystalline</a> silvery-white <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metal</a>. When a bar of tin is bent, a crackling sound known as the "<a href="/wiki/Tin_cry" title="Tin cry">tin cry</a>" can be heard from the <a href="/wiki/Crystal_twinning" title="Crystal twinning">twinning</a> of the crystals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hol1985_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hol1985-6">[6]</a></sup> Tin melts at low temperatures of about 232 °C (450 °F), the lowest in group 14. The melting point is further lowered to 177.3 °C (351.1 °F) for 11 nm particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 240px; float: left; clear: left; margin:0 1.5em 1.5em 0;"><caption>External video</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left">
<img alt="" data-file-height="128" data-file-width="128" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> <a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=sXB83Heh3_c" rel="nofollow">β–α transition of tin</a> at −40 °C (time lapse; one second of the video is one hour in real time</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>β-tin (the metallic form, or white tin, BCT structure), which is stable at and above room temperature, is malleable. In contrast, α-tin (nonmetallic form, or gray tin), which is stable below 13.2 °C (55.8 °F), is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brittle" title="Brittle">brittle</a>. α-tin has a <a href="/wiki/Diamond_cubic" title="Diamond cubic">diamond cubic</a> <a href="/wiki/Crystal_structure" title="Crystal structure">crystal structure</a>, similar to <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> or <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a>. α-tin has no metallic properties at all because its atoms form a covalent structure in which electrons cannot move freely. It is a dull-gray powdery material with no common uses other than a few specialized <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductor</a> applications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hol1985_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hol1985-6">[6]</a></sup> These two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allotrope" title="Allotrope">allotropes</a>, α-tin and β-tin, are more commonly known as <i>gray tin</i> and <i>white tin</i>, respectively. Two more allotropes, γ and σ, exist at temperatures above 161 °C (322 °F)  and pressures above several <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">GPa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> In cold conditions, β-tin tends to transform spontaneously into α-tin, a phenomenon known as "<a href="/wiki/Tin_pest" title="Tin pest">tin pest</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Although the α-β transformation temperature is nominally 13.2 °C (55.8 °F), impurities (e.g. Al, Zn, etc.) lower the transition temperature well below 0 °C (32 °F) and, on the addition of <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">bismuth</a>, the transformation might not occur at all, increasing the durability of the tin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwartz_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schwartz-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Commercial grades of tin (99.8%) resist transformation because of the inhibiting effect of the small amounts of bismuth, antimony, lead, and silver present as impurities. Alloying elements such as copper, antimony, bismuth, cadmium, and silver increase its hardness. Tin tends rather easily to form hard, brittle intermetallic phases, which are often undesirable. It does not form wide solid solution ranges in other metals in general, and few elements have appreciable solid solubility in tin. Simple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eutectic_point" title="Eutectic point">eutectic</a> systems, however, occur with <a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">bismuth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gallium" title="Gallium">gallium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thallium" title="Thallium">thallium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwartz_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schwartz-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tin becomes a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superconductor" title="Superconductor">superconductor</a> below 3.72 <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> and was one of the first superconductors to be studied; the <a href="/wiki/Meissner_effect" title="Meissner effect">Meissner effect</a>, one of the characteristic features of superconductors, was first discovered in superconducting tin crystals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meissner1_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-meissner1-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical">Chemical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Chemical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Tin resists corrosion from <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>, but can be attacked by <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alkali" title="Alkali">alkalis</a>. Tin can be highly polished and is used as a protective coat for other metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hol1985_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hol1985-6">[6]</a></sup> A protective oxide (<a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">passivation</a>) layer prevents further oxidation, the same that forms on pewter and other tin alloys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Tin acts as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a> when <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> is in solution and helps to accelerate the chemical reaction.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="not clear what it accelerates chemical attack of (August 2015)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hol1985_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hol1985-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_tin" title="Isotopes of tin">Isotopes of tin</a></div>
<p>Tin has ten <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stable_isotopes" title="Stable isotopes">stable isotopes</a>, with atomic masses of 112, 114 through 120, 122 and 124, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_elements_by_stability_of_isotopes" title="List of elements by stability of isotopes">greatest number</a> of any element. Of these, the most abundant are <sup>120</sup>Sn (almost a third of all tin), <sup>118</sup>Sn, and <sup>116</sup>Sn, while the least abundant is <sup>115</sup>Sn. The isotopes with even <a href="/wiki/Mass_number" title="Mass number">mass numbers</a> have no <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_spin" title="Nuclear spin">nuclear spin</a>, while those with odd have a spin of +1/2. Tin, with its three common isotopes <sup>116</sup>Sn, <sup>118</sup>Sn and <sup>120</sup>Sn, is among the easiest elements to detect and analyze by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NMR_spectroscopy" title="NMR spectroscopy">NMR spectroscopy</a>, and its <a href="/wiki/Chemical_shift" title="Chemical shift">chemical shifts</a> are referenced against <span class="chemf nowrap">SnMe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[note 1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>This large number of stable isotopes is thought to be a direct result of the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 50, a "<a href="/wiki/Magic_number_(physics)" title="Magic number (physics)">magic number</a>" in nuclear physics. Tin also occurs in 29 unstable isotopes, encompassing all the remaining atomic masses from 99 to 137. Apart from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tin-126" title="Tin-126"><sup>126</sup>Sn</a>, with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 230,000 years, all the radioisotopes have a half-life of less than a year. The radioactive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tin-100" title="Tin-100"><sup>100</sup>Sn</a>, discovered in 1994, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tin-132" title="Tin-132"><sup>132</sup>Sn</a> are one of the few nuclides with a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double_magic" title="Double magic">doubly magic</a>" nucleus: despite being unstable, having very lopsided proton-neutron ratios, they represent endpoints beyond which stability drops off rapidly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> Another 30 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metastable_isomers" title="Metastable isomers">metastable isomers</a> have been characterized for isotopes between 111 and 131, the most stable being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tin-121m" title="Tin-121m"><sup>121m</sup>Sn</a> with a half-life of 43.9 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Audi_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Audi-17">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>The relative differences in the abundances of tin's stable isotopes can be explained by their different modes of formation in <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a>. <sup>116</sup>Sn through <sup>120</sup>Sn inclusive are formed in the <a href="/wiki/S-process" title="S-process">s-process</a> (slow neutron capture) in most <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> and hence they are the most common isotopes, while <sup>122</sup>Sn and <sup>124</sup>Sn are only formed in the <a href="/wiki/R-process" title="R-process">r-process</a> (rapid neutron capture) in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supernovae" title="Supernovae">supernovae</a> and are less common. (The isotopes <sup>117</sup>Sn through <sup>120</sup>Sn also receive contributions from the r-process.) Finally, the rarest proton-rich isotopes, <sup>112</sup>Sn, <sup>114</sup>Sn, and <sup>115</sup>Sn, cannot be made in significant amounts in the s- or r-processes and are considered among the <a href="/wiki/P-nuclei" title="P-nuclei">p-nuclei</a>, whose origins are not well understood yet. Some speculated mechanisms for their formation include proton capture as well as <a href="/wiki/Photodisintegration" title="Photodisintegration">photodisintegration</a>, although <sup>115</sup>Sn might also be partially produced in the s-process, both directly, and as the daughter of long-lived <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_indium" title="Isotopes of indium"><sup>115</sup>In</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cameron_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cameron-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The word <i>tin</i> is shared among <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic languages</a> and can be traced back to <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_reconstruction" title="Linguistic reconstruction">reconstructed</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" title="Proto-Germanic">Proto-Germanic</a> <i>*tin-om</i>; <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">cognates</a> include <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> <i><i lang="de" title="German language text">Zinn</i></i>, <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">Swedish</a> <i><i lang="sv" title="Swedish language text">tenn</i></i> and <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a> <i><i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">tin</i></i>. It is not found in other branches of <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a>, except by <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">borrowing</a> from Germanic (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a> <i><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">tinne</i></i> from English).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oed_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-oed-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a> name <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">stannum</i></i> originally meant an alloy of silver and lead, and came to mean 'tin' in the 4th century<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup>—the earlier Latin word for it was <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">plumbum candidum</i></i>, or "white lead". <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Stannum</i></i> apparently came from an earlier <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">stāgnum</i></i> (meaning the same substance),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oed_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-oed-19">[18]</a></sup> the origin of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romance_language" title="Romance language">Romance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic</a> terms for <i>tin</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oed_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-oed-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> The origin of <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">stannum</i></i>/<i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">stāgnum</i></i> is unknown; it may be pre-<a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i><i lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/Meyers_Konversations-Lexikon" title="Meyers Konversations-Lexikon">Meyers Konversations-Lexikon</a></i></i> speculates on the contrary that <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">stannum</i></i> is derived from (the ancestor of) <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish</a> <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">stean</i></i>, and is proof that <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a> in the first centuries AD was the main source of tin.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tin_sources_and_trade_in_ancient_times" title="Tin sources and trade in ancient times">Tin sources and trade in ancient times</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sword_bronze_age_(2nd_version).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1000" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Sword_bronze_age_%282nd_version%29.jpg/220px-Sword_bronze_age_%282nd_version%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Sword_bronze_age_%282nd_version%29.jpg/330px-Sword_bronze_age_%282nd_version%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Sword_bronze_age_%282nd_version%29.jpg/440px-Sword_bronze_age_%282nd_version%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sword_bronze_age_(2nd_version).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ceremonial giant bronze <a href="/wiki/Dirk" title="Dirk">dirk</a> of the Plougrescant-Ommerschans type, Plougrescant, France, 1500–1300 BC.</div></div></div>
<p>Tin extraction and use can be dated to the beginnings of the Bronze Age around 3000 BC, when it was observed that <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> objects formed of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polymetallic" title="Polymetallic">polymetallic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ores" title="Ores">ores</a> with different metal contents had different physical properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup> The earliest bronze objects had a tin or arsenic content of less than 2% and are therefore believed to be the result of unintentional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alloying" title="Alloying">alloying</a> due to trace metal content in the copper ore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pen1986_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-pen1986-25">[24]</a></sup> The addition of a second metal to copper increases its hardness, lowers the melting temperature, and improves the <a href="/wiki/Casting" title="Casting">casting</a> process by producing a more fluid melt that cools to a denser, less spongy metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pen1986_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-pen1986-25">[24]</a></sup> This was an important innovation that allowed for the much more complex shapes cast in closed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mold_(manufacturing)" title="Mold (manufacturing)">moulds</a> of the Bronze Age. <a href="/wiki/Arsenical_bronze" title="Arsenical bronze">Arsenical bronze</a> objects appear first in the Near East where arsenic is commonly found in association with copper ore, but the <a href="/wiki/Arsenic_poisoning" title="Arsenic poisoning">health risks</a> were quickly realized and the quest for sources of the much less hazardous tin ores began early in the Bronze Age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> This created the demand for rare tin metal and formed a <a href="/wiki/Trade" title="Trade">trade</a> network that linked the distant sources of tin to the markets of Bronze Age <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cultures" title="Cultures">cultures</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cassiterite" title="Cassiterite">Cassiterite</a> (SnO<sub>2</sub>), the tin oxide form of tin, was most likely the original source of tin in ancient times. Other forms of tin ores are less abundant <a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">sulfides</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Stannite" title="Stannite">stannite</a> that require a more involved <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelting</a> process. Cassiterite often accumulates in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alluvial" title="Alluvial">alluvial</a> channels as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Placer_deposits" title="Placer deposits">placer deposits</a> because it is harder, heavier, and more chemically resistant than the accompanying <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> Cassiterite is usually black or generally dark in color, and these deposits can be easily seen in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/River_banks" title="River banks">river banks</a>. Alluvial (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Placer_deposits" title="Placer deposits">placer</a>) deposits could be easily collected and separated by methods similar to <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a> <a href="/wiki/Gold_panning" title="Gold panning">panning</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds_and_chemistry">Compounds and chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Compounds and chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Tin_compounds" title="Category:Tin compounds">Category:Tin compounds</a>.</div>
<p>In the great majority of its compounds, tin has the oxidation state II or IV.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inorganic_compounds">Inorganic compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Inorganic compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Halide compounds are known for both oxidation states. For Sn(IV), all four halides are well known: <a href="/wiki/Tin(IV)_fluoride" title="Tin(IV) fluoride">SnF<sub>4</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Tin(IV)_chloride" title="Tin(IV) chloride">SnCl<sub>4</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Tin(IV)_bromide" title="Tin(IV) bromide">SnBr<sub>4</sub></a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tin(IV)_iodide" title="Tin(IV) iodide">SnI<sub>4</sub></a>. The three heavier members are volatile molecular compounds, whereas the tetrafluoride is polymeric. All four halides are known for Sn(II) also: <a href="/wiki/Tin(II)_fluoride" title="Tin(II) fluoride">SnF<sub>2</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Tin(II)_chloride" title="Tin(II) chloride">SnCl<sub>2</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Tin(II)_bromide" title="Tin(II) bromide">SnBr<sub>2</sub></a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tin(II)_iodide" title="Tin(II) iodide">SnI<sub>2</sub></a>. All are polymeric solids. Of these eight compounds, only the iodides are colored.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wiberg&amp;Holleman_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wiberg&amp;Holleman-28">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Tin(II)_chloride" title="Tin(II) chloride">Tin(II) chloride</a> (also known as stannous chloride) is the most important tin halide in a commercial sense. Illustrating the routes to such compounds, <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> reacts with tin metal to give SnCl<sub>4</sub> whereas the reaction of hydrochloric acid and tin produces SnCl<sub>2</sub> and hydrogen gas. Alternatively SnCl<sub>4</sub> and Sn combine to stannous chloride by a process called <a href="/wiki/Comproportionation" title="Comproportionation">comproportionation</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>SnCl<sub>4</sub> + Sn → 2 SnCl<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>Tin can form many oxides, sulfides, and other chalcogenide derivatives. The dioxide SnO<sub>2</sub> (cassiterite) forms when tin is heated in the presence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air" title="Air">air</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wiberg&amp;Holleman_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wiberg&amp;Holleman-28">[27]</a></sup> SnO<sub>2</sub> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphoteric" title="Amphoteric">amphoteric</a>, which means that it dissolves in both acidic and basic solutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherwood_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sherwood-30">[29]</a></sup> Stannates with the structure [Sn(OH)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>2−</sup>, like K<sub>2</sub>[Sn(OH)<sub>6</sub>], are also known, though the free stannic acid H<sub>2</sub>[Sn(OH)<sub>6</sub>] is unknown.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">Sulfides</a> of tin exist in both the +2 and +4 oxidation states: <a href="/wiki/Tin(II)_sulfide" title="Tin(II) sulfide">tin(II) sulfide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tin(IV)_sulfide" title="Tin(IV) sulfide">tin(IV) sulfide</a> (<a href="/wiki/Mosaic_gold" title="Mosaic gold">mosaic gold</a>).
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tin(II)-chloride-xtal-1996-3D-balls-front.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1005" data-file-width="2000" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Tin%28II%29-chloride-xtal-1996-3D-balls-front.png/220px-Tin%28II%29-chloride-xtal-1996-3D-balls-front.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Tin%28II%29-chloride-xtal-1996-3D-balls-front.png/330px-Tin%28II%29-chloride-xtal-1996-3D-balls-front.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Tin%28II%29-chloride-xtal-1996-3D-balls-front.png/440px-Tin%28II%29-chloride-xtal-1996-3D-balls-front.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tin(II)-chloride-xtal-1996-3D-balls-front.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ball-and-stick_model" title="Ball-and-stick model">Ball-and-stick models</a> of the structure of solid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stannous_chloride" title="Stannous chloride">stannous chloride</a> (SnCl<sub>2</sub>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrides">Hydrides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Hydrides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Stannane" title="Stannane">Stannane</a> (SnH<sub>4</sub>), with tin in the +4 oxidation state, is unstable. Organotin hydrides are however well known, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Tributyltin_hydride" title="Tributyltin hydride">tributyltin hydride</a> (Sn(C<sub>4</sub>H<sub>9</sub>)<sub>3</sub>H).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hol1985_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-Hol1985-6">[6]</a></sup> These compound release transient tributyl tin radicals, which are rare examples of compounds of tin(III).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organotin_compounds">Organotin compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Organotin compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organotin" title="Organotin">Organotin</a> compounds, sometimes called stannanes, are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_compounds" title="Chemical compounds">chemical compounds</a> with tin–carbon bonds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> Of the compounds of tin, the organic derivatives are the most useful commercially.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-34">[33]</a></sup> Some organotin compounds are highly toxic and have been used as <a href="/wiki/Biocide" title="Biocide">biocides</a>. The first organotin compound to be reported was diethyltin diiodide ((C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>5</sub>)<sub>2</sub>SnI<sub>2</sub>), reported by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Frankland" title="Edward Frankland">Edward Frankland</a> in 1849.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most organotin compounds are colorless liquids or solids that are stable to air and water. They adopt tetrahedral geometry. Tetraalkyl- and tetraaryltin compounds can be prepared using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grignard_reagent" title="Grignard reagent">Grignard reagents</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-34">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">SnCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + 4 RMgBr → <span class="chemf nowrap">R<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>Sn</span> + 4 MgBrCl</dd></dl>
<p>The mixed halide-alkyls, which are more common and more important commercially than the tetraorgano derivatives, are prepared by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Redistribution_reaction" title="Redistribution reaction">redistribution reactions</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">SnCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">R<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>Sn</span> → 2 SnCl<sub>2</sub>R<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>Divalent organotin compounds are uncommon, although more common than related divalent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organogermanium" title="Organogermanium">organogermanium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Organosilicon" title="Organosilicon">organosilicon</a> compounds. The greater stabilization enjoyed by Sn(II) is attributed to the "<a href="/wiki/Inert_pair_effect" title="Inert pair effect">inert pair effect</a>". Organotin(II) compounds include both stannylenes (formula: R<sub>2</sub>Sn, as seen for singlet <a href="/wiki/Carbene" title="Carbene">carbenes</a>) and distannylenes (R<sub>4</sub>Sn<sub>2</sub>), which are roughly equivalent to <a href="/wiki/Alkene" title="Alkene">alkenes</a>. Both classes exhibit unusual reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Tin_minerals" title="Category:Tin minerals">Category:Tin minerals</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cassiterite09.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="350" data-file-width="574" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Cassiterite09.jpg/200px-Cassiterite09.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Cassiterite09.jpg/300px-Cassiterite09.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Cassiterite09.jpg/400px-Cassiterite09.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cassiterite09.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sample of cassiterite, the main <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ore</a> of tin.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TinOreUSGOV.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="451" data-file-width="600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/TinOreUSGOV.jpg/220px-TinOreUSGOV.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/TinOreUSGOV.jpg/330px-TinOreUSGOV.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/TinOreUSGOV.jpg/440px-TinOreUSGOV.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TinOreUSGOV.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Granular pieces of cassiterite, collected by <a href="/wiki/Placer_mining" title="Placer mining">placer mining</a></div></div></div>
<p>Tin is generated via the long <a href="/wiki/S-process" title="S-process">s-process</a> in low-to-medium mass stars (with masses of 0.6 to 10 times that of <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>), and finally by <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a> of the heavy isotopes of <a href="/wiki/Indium" title="Indium">indium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tin is the 49th most abundant element in <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crust</a>, representing 2 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Part_per_million" title="Part per million">ppm</a> compared with 75 ppm for zinc, 50 ppm for copper, and 14 ppm for lead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tin does not occur as the native element but must be extracted from various ores. <a href="/wiki/Cassiterite" title="Cassiterite">Cassiterite</a> (SnO<sub>2</sub>) is the only commercially important source of tin, although small quantities of tin are recovered from complex <a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">sulfides</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Stannite" title="Stannite">stannite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cylindrite" title="Cylindrite">cylindrite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Franckeite" title="Franckeite">franckeite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canfieldite" title="Canfieldite">canfieldite</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Teallite" title="Teallite">teallite</a>. Minerals with tin are almost always associated with <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granite</a> rock, usually at a level of 1% tin oxide content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-I230527_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-I230527-39">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of the higher specific gravity of tin dioxide, about 80% of mined tin is from secondary deposits found downstream from the primary lodes. Tin is often recovered from granules washed downstream in the past and deposited in valleys or the sea. The most economical ways of mining tin are by <a href="/wiki/Dredging" title="Dredging">dredging</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_mining" title="Hydraulic mining">hydraulicking</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_cast_mining" title="Open cast mining">open pits</a>. Most of the world's tin is produced from <a href="/wiki/Placer_mining" title="Placer mining">placer</a> deposits, which can contain as little as 0.015% tin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p>

<p>About 253,000 tonnes of tin have been mined in 2011, mostly in China (110,000 t), Indonesia (51,000 t), Peru (34,600 t), Bolivia (20,700 t) and Brazil (12,000 t).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS200YB_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-USGS200YB-41">[40]</a></sup> Estimates of tin production have historically varied with the dynamics of economic feasibility and the development of mining technologies, but it is estimated that, at current consumption rates and technologies, the Earth will run out of mine-able tin in 40 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lester_R._Brown" title="Lester R. Brown">Lester Brown</a> has suggested tin could run out within 20 years based on an extremely conservative extrapolation of 2% growth per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-43">[42]</a></sup>
</p>

<p>Secondary, or scrap, tin is also an important source of the metal. Recovery of tin through secondary production, or recycling of scrap tin, is increasing rapidly. Whereas the United States has neither mined since 1993 nor smelted tin since 1989, it was the largest secondary producer, recycling nearly 14,000 tonnes in 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS200YB_41-4"><a href="#cite_note-USGS200YB-41">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>New deposits are reported in southern <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> and in 2009, new deposits of tin were discovered in Colombia by the Seminole Group Colombia CI, SAS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Tin is produced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbothermic_reduction" title="Carbothermic reduction">carbothermic reduction</a> of the oxide <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ore</a> with <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> or coke. Both <a href="/wiki/Reverberatory_furnace" title="Reverberatory furnace">reverberatory furnace</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electric_arc_furnace" title="Electric arc furnace">electric furnace</a> can be used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mining_and_smelting">Mining and smelting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Mining and smelting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tin_mining" title="Tin mining">Tin mining</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industry">Industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Candlestick_made_of_Tin_by_Royal_Selangor.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Candlestick_made_of_Tin_by_Royal_Selangor.JPG/220px-Candlestick_made_of_Tin_by_Royal_Selangor.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Candlestick_made_of_Tin_by_Royal_Selangor.JPG/330px-Candlestick_made_of_Tin_by_Royal_Selangor.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Candlestick_made_of_Tin_by_Royal_Selangor.JPG/440px-Candlestick_made_of_Tin_by_Royal_Selangor.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Candlestick_made_of_Tin_by_Royal_Selangor.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Candlestick made of tin</div></div></div>
<p>The ten largest companies produced most of the world's tin in 2007.
</p><p>Most of the world's tin is traded on the <a href="/wiki/London_Metal_Exchange" title="London Metal Exchange">London Metal Exchange</a> (LME), from 8 countries, under 17 brands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup>
</p>

<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p>An <a href="/wiki/International_Tin_Council" title="International Tin Council">International Tin Council</a> was established in 1947 to control the price of tin, until it collapsed in 1985. In 1984, an <i>Association of Tin Producing Countries</i> was created, with Australia, Bolivia, Indonesia, Malaysia, Nigeria, Thailand, and Zaire as members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Price_and_exchanges">Price and exchanges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Price and exchanges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SnPrice.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="940" data-file-width="1400" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/SnPrice.png/220px-SnPrice.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/SnPrice.png/330px-SnPrice.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/SnPrice.png/440px-SnPrice.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SnPrice.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World production and price (US exchange) of tin.</div></div></div>
<p>Tin is unique among other mineral commodities because of the complex agreements between producer countries and consumer countries dating back to 1921. The earlier agreements tended to be somewhat informal and sporadic and led to the "First International Tin Agreement" in 1956, the first of a continuously numbered series that effectively collapsed in 1985. Through this series of agreements, the <a href="/wiki/International_Tin_Council" title="International Tin Council">International Tin Council</a> (ITC) had a considerable effect on tin prices. The ITC supported the price of tin during periods of low prices by buying tin for its buffer stockpile and was able to restrain the price during periods of high prices by selling tin from the stockpile. This was an anti-free-market approach, designed to assure a sufficient flow of tin to consumer countries and a profit for producer countries. However, the buffer stockpile was not sufficiently large, and during most of those 29 years tin prices rose, sometimes sharply, especially from 1973 through 1980 when rampant inflation plagued many world economies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-price_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-price-52">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the late 1970s and early 1980s, the U.S. Government tin stockpile was in an aggressive selling mode, partly to take advantage of the historically high tin prices. The sharp recession of 1981–82 proved to be quite harsh on the tin industry. Tin consumption declined dramatically. The ITC was able to avoid truly steep declines through accelerated buying for its buffer stockpile; this activity required the ITC to borrow extensively from banks and metal trading firms to augment its resources. The ITC continued to borrow until late 1985 when it reached its credit limit. Immediately, a major "tin crisis" followed — tin was delisted from trading on the London Metal Exchange for about three years, the ITC dissolved soon afterward, and the price of tin, now in a free-market environment, plummeted sharply to $4 per pound and remained at that level through the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-price_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-price-52">[51]</a></sup> The price increased again by 2010 with a rebound in consumption following the 2008–09 world economic crisis, accompanying restocking and continued growth in consumption by the world's developing economies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS200YB_41-5"><a href="#cite_note-USGS200YB-41">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/London_Metal_Exchange" title="London Metal Exchange">London Metal Exchange</a> (LME) is the principal trading site for tin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS200YB_41-6"><a href="#cite_note-USGS200YB-41">[40]</a></sup> Other tin contract markets are <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kuala_Lumpur_Tin_Market&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kuala Lumpur Tin Market (page does not exist)">Kuala Lumpur Tin Market</a> (KLTM) and <a href="/wiki/INATIN" title="INATIN">Indonesia Tin Exchange</a> (INATIN).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>The price per kg over years:
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Tin (US$ per kg)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tin_(USD_cents_per_kg)_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tin_(USD_cents_per_kg)-54">[53]</a></sup>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TinConsChart.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="359" data-file-width="419" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/35/TinConsChart.jpg/220px-TinConsChart.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/35/TinConsChart.jpg/330px-TinConsChart.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/3/35/TinConsChart.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TinConsChart.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World consumption of refined tin by end use, 2006</div></div></div>
<p>In 2006, about half of all tin produced was used in solder. The rest was divided between tin plating, tin chemicals, brass and bronze alloys, and niche uses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solder">Solder</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Solder">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ex_Lead_freesolder.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="140" data-file-width="200" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a4/Ex_Lead_freesolder.jpg" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ex_Lead_freesolder.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A coil of lead-free <a href="/wiki/Solder" title="Solder">solder</a> wire</div></div></div>
<p>Tin has long been used in alloys with lead as <a href="/wiki/Solder" title="Solder">solder</a>, in amounts 5 to 70% w/w. Tin with lead forms a <a href="/wiki/Eutectic_system" title="Eutectic system">eutectic mixture</a> at the weight proportion of 61.9% tin and 38.1% lead (the atomic proportion: 73.9% tin and 26.1% lead), with melting temperature of 183 °C (361.4 °F) . Such solders are primarily used for joining <a href="/wiki/Plumbing" title="Plumbing">pipes</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_circuit" title="Electric circuit">electric circuits</a>. Since the European Union <a href="/wiki/Waste_Electrical_and_Electronic_Equipment_Directive" title="Waste Electrical and Electronic Equipment Directive">Waste Electrical and Electronic Equipment Directive</a> (WEEE Directive) and <a href="/wiki/Restriction_of_Hazardous_Substances_Directive" title="Restriction of Hazardous Substances Directive">Restriction of Hazardous Substances Directive</a> came into effect on 1 July 2006, the lead content in such alloys has decreased. Replacing lead has many problems, including a higher melting point, and the formation of <a href="/wiki/Whisker_(metallurgy)" title="Whisker (metallurgy)">tin whiskers</a> causing electrical problems. <a href="/wiki/Tin_pest" title="Tin pest">Tin pest</a> can occur in lead-free solders, leading to loss of the soldered joint. Replacement alloys are rapidly being found, although problems of joint integrity remain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tin_plating">Tin plating</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Tin plating">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Tin bonds readily to <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> and is used for coating <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, zinc and steel to prevent corrosion. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tin_plating" title="Tin plating">Tin-plated</a> steel containers are widely used for <a href="/wiki/Food_preservation" title="Food preservation">food preservation</a>, and this forms a large part of the market for metallic tin. A tinplate canister for preserving food was first manufactured in London in 1812.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> Speakers of British English call them "tins", while speakers of American English call them "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tin_cans" title="Tin cans">cans</a>" or "tin cans". One derivation of such use is the slang term "<a href="/wiki/Tinnie" title="Tinnie">tinnie</a>" or "tinny", meaning "can of beer" in <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Tin_whistle" title="Tin whistle">tin whistle</a> is so called because it was first mass-produced in tin-plated steel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup> Copper cooking vessels such as saucepans and frying pans are frequently lined with a thin plating of tin, since the combination of acid foods with copper can be toxic.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Specialized_alloys">Specialized alloys</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Specialized alloys">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pewterplate_exb.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="283" data-file-width="432" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Pewterplate_exb.jpg/220px-Pewterplate_exb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Pewterplate_exb.jpg/330px-Pewterplate_exb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/91/Pewterplate_exb.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pewterplate_exb.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pewter" title="Pewter">Pewter</a> plate</div></div></div>
<p>Tin in combination with other elements forms a wide variety of useful alloys. Tin is most commonly alloyed with copper. <a href="/wiki/Pewter" title="Pewter">Pewter</a> is 85–99% tin;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babbitt_metal" title="Babbitt metal">bearing metal</a> has a high percentage of tin as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">Bronze</a> is mostly copper (12% tin), while addition of phosphorus gives <a href="/wiki/Phosphor_bronze" title="Phosphor bronze">phosphor bronze</a>. <a href="/wiki/Bell_metal" title="Bell metal">Bell metal</a> is also a copper-tin alloy, containing 22% tin. Tin has sometimes been used in coinage; for example, it once formed a single-digit percentage (usually five percent or less) of American<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> and Canadian<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup> pennies. Because copper is often the major metal in such coins, sometimes including zinc, these could be called bronze and/or brass alloys.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inside_of_a_tin_platted_can.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Inside_of_a_tin_platted_can.jpg/220px-Inside_of_a_tin_platted_can.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Inside_of_a_tin_platted_can.jpg/330px-Inside_of_a_tin_platted_can.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Inside_of_a_tin_platted_can.jpg/440px-Inside_of_a_tin_platted_can.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inside_of_a_tin_platted_can.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tin plated metal from a <a href="/wiki/Tin_can" title="Tin can">can</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alfonso_Santiago_Leyva_and_his_son_Toma%C2%A6%C3%BCs_working.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="2100" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Alfonso_Santiago_Leyva_and_his_son_Toma%C2%A6%C3%BCs_working.jpg/220px-Alfonso_Santiago_Leyva_and_his_son_Toma%C2%A6%C3%BCs_working.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Alfonso_Santiago_Leyva_and_his_son_Toma%C2%A6%C3%BCs_working.jpg/330px-Alfonso_Santiago_Leyva_and_his_son_Toma%C2%A6%C3%BCs_working.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Alfonso_Santiago_Leyva_and_his_son_Toma%C2%A6%C3%BCs_working.jpg/440px-Alfonso_Santiago_Leyva_and_his_son_Toma%C2%A6%C3%BCs_working.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alfonso_Santiago_Leyva_and_his_son_Toma%C2%A6%C3%BCs_working.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artisan Alfonso Santiago Leyva and his son working with tin sheets.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Niobium" title="Niobium">niobium</a>-tin compound <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Niobium-tin" title="Niobium-tin">Nb<sub>3</sub>Sn</a> is commercially used in <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_coil" title="Electromagnetic coil">coils</a> of <a href="/wiki/Superconducting_magnet" title="Superconducting magnet">superconducting magnets</a> for its high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critical_temperature#In_Superconductivity" title="Critical temperature">critical temperature</a> (18 K) and critical magnetic field (25 <a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">T</a>). A superconducting magnet weighing as little as two <a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">kilograms</a> is capable of the magnetic field of a conventional <a href="/wiki/Electromagnet" title="Electromagnet">electromagnet</a> weighing tons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geballe_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-geballe-65">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>A small percentage of tin is added to <a href="/wiki/Zirconium_alloy" title="Zirconium alloy">zirconium alloys</a> for the cladding of nuclear fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most metal pipes in a <a href="/wiki/Pipe_organ" title="Pipe organ">pipe organ</a> are of a tin/lead alloy, with 50/50 being the most common composition. The proportion of tin in the pipe defines the pipe's tone, since tin has a desirable tonal resonance. When a tin/lead alloy cools, the lead cools slightly faster and produces a mottled or spotted effect. This metal alloy is referred to as spotted metal. Major advantages of using tin for pipes include its appearance, its workability, and resistance to corrosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Optoelectronics">Optoelectronics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Optoelectronics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Indium_tin_oxide" title="Indium tin oxide">oxides of indium and tin</a> are electrically conductive and transparent, and are used to make transparent electrically conducting films with applications in <a href="/wiki/Optoelectronics" title="Optoelectronics">Optoelectronics</a> devices such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_crystal_displays" title="Liquid crystal displays">liquid crystal displays</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kimetal_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kimetal-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_applications">Other applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Other applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Punched_tin_barn_lantern.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="600" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Punched_tin_barn_lantern.jpeg/220px-Punched_tin_barn_lantern.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Punched_tin_barn_lantern.jpeg/330px-Punched_tin_barn_lantern.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Punched_tin_barn_lantern.jpeg/440px-Punched_tin_barn_lantern.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Punched_tin_barn_lantern.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 21st-century reproduction barn lantern made of punched tin.</div></div></div>
<p>Punched tin-plated steel, also called pierced tin, is an artisan technique originating in central Europe for creating housewares that are both functional and decorative. Decorative piercing designs exist in a wide variety, based on local tradition and the artisan's personal creations. Punched tin lanterns are the most common application of this artisan technique. The light of a candle shining through the pierced design creates a decorative light pattern in the room where it sits. Lanterns and other punched tin articles were created in the New World from the earliest European settlement. A well-known example is the Revere lantern, named after <a href="/wiki/Paul_Revere" title="Paul Revere">Paul Revere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Before the modern era, in some areas of the Alps, a goat or sheep's horn would be sharpened and a tin panel would be punched out using the alphabet and numbers from one to nine. This learning tool was known appropriately as "the horn". Modern reproductions are decorated with such motifs as hearts and tulips.
</p><p>In America, <a href="/wiki/Pie_safe" title="Pie safe">pie safes</a> and food safes were in use in the days before refrigeration. These were wooden cupboards of various styles and sizes – either floor standing or hanging cupboards meant to discourage vermin and insects and to keep dust from perishable foodstuffs. These cabinets had tinplate inserts in the doors and sometimes in the sides, punched out by the homeowner, cabinetmaker or a tinsmith in varying designs to allow for air circulation while excluding flies. Modern reproductions of these articles remain popular in North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>Window glass is most often made by floating molten <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glass</a> on molten tin (<a href="/wiki/Float_glass" title="Float glass">float glass</a>), resulting in a flat and flawless surface. This is also called the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pilkington_process" title="Pilkington process">Pilkington process</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tin is also used as a negative electrode in advanced Li-ion batteries. Its application is somewhat limited by the fact that some tin surfaces<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (June 2013)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> catalyze decomposition of carbonate-based electrolytes used in Li-ion batteries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Tin(II)_fluoride" title="Tin(II) fluoride">Tin(II) fluoride</a> is added to some dental care products<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup> as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stannous_fluoride" title="Stannous fluoride">stannous fluoride</a> (SnF<sub>2</sub>). <a href="/wiki/Tin(II)_fluoride" title="Tin(II) fluoride">Tin(II) fluoride</a> can be mixed with calcium abrasives while the more common <a href="/wiki/Sodium_fluoride" title="Sodium fluoride">sodium fluoride</a> gradually becomes biologically inactive in the presence of calcium compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> It has also been shown to be more effective than <a href="/wiki/Sodium_fluoride" title="Sodium fluoride">sodium fluoride</a> in controlling <a href="/wiki/Gingivitis" title="Gingivitis">gingivitis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organotin_compounds_2">Organotin compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Organotin compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Organotin_chemistry" title="Organotin chemistry">Organotin chemistry</a></div>
<p>Of all the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">chemical compounds</a> of tin, the organotin compounds are most heavily used. Worldwide industrial production probably exceeds 50,000 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="PVC_stabilizers">PVC stabilizers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: PVC stabilizers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The major commercial application of organotin compounds is in the stabilization of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PVC" title="PVC">PVC</a> plastics. In the absence of such stabilizers, PVC would otherwise rapidly degrade under heat, light, and atmospheric oxygen, resulting in discolored, brittle products. Tin scavenges labile <a href="/wiki/Chloride" title="Chloride">chloride</a> ions (Cl<sup>−</sup>), which would otherwise initiate loss of HCl from the plastic material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atkins_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Atkins-78">[77]</a></sup> Typical tin compounds are carboxylic acid derivatives of dibutyltin dichloride, such as the di<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laurate" title="Laurate">laurate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Biocides">Biocides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Biocides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some organotin compounds are relatively toxic, with both advantages and problems. They are used for <a href="/wiki/Biocide" title="Biocide">biocidal properties</a> as <a href="/wiki/Fungicide" title="Fungicide">fungicides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algaecide" title="Algaecide">algaecides</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wood_preservative" title="Wood preservative">wood preservatives</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antifouling_agent" title="Antifouling agent">antifouling agents</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atkins_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Atkins-78">[77]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tributyltin_oxide" title="Tributyltin oxide">Tributyltin oxide</a> is used as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wood_preservative" title="Wood preservative">wood preservative</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tributyltin" title="Tributyltin">Tributyltin</a> was used as additive for ship paint to prevent growth of marine organisms on ships, with use declining after organotin compounds were recognized as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persistent_organic_pollutants" title="Persistent organic pollutants">persistent organic pollutants</a> with an extremely high toxicity for some marine organisms (the <a href="/wiki/Dog_whelk" title="Dog whelk">dog whelk</a>, for example).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup> The EU banned the use of organotin compounds in 2003,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup> while concerns over the toxicity of these compounds to marine life and damage to the reproduction and growth of some marine species<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atkins_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-Atkins-78">[77]</a></sup> (some reports describe biological effects to marine life at a concentration of 1 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanogram" title="Nanogram">nanogram</a> per liter) have led to a worldwide ban by the <a href="/wiki/International_Maritime_Organization" title="International Maritime Organization">International Maritime Organization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup> Many nations now restrict the use of organotin compounds to vessels greater than 25 m (82 ft) long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atkins_78-3"><a href="#cite_note-Atkins-78">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Organic_chemistry">Organic chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Organic chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some tin <a href="/wiki/Reagent" title="Reagent">reagents</a> are useful in <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a>. In the largest application, stannous chloride is a common reducing agent for the conversion of <a href="/wiki/Nitro_compound" title="Nitro compound">nitro</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oxime" title="Oxime">oxime</a> groups to <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">amines</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Stille_reaction" title="Stille reaction">Stille reaction</a> couples organotin compounds with organic <a href="/wiki/Halide" title="Halide">halides</a> or <a href="/wiki/Pseudohalogen" title="Pseudohalogen">pseudohalides</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Li-ion_batteries">Li-ion batteries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Li-ion batteries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Tin forms several inter-metallic phases with lithium metal, making it a potentially attractive material for battery applications. Large volumetric expansion of tin upon alloying with lithium and instability of the tin-organic electrolyte interface at low electrochemical potentials are the greatest challenges to employment in commercial cells. The problem was partially solved by Sony. Tin inter-metallic compound with cobalt and carbon has been implemented by Sony in its Nexelion cells released in the late 2000s. The composition of the active material is approximately Sn<sub>0.3</sub>Co<sub>0.4</sub>C<sub>0.3</sub>. Recent research showed that only some crystalline facets of tetragonal (beta) Sn are responsible for undesirable electrochemical activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Precautions">Precautions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Precautions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tin_poisoning" title="Tin poisoning">Tin poisoning</a></div>
<p>Cases of poisoning from tin metal, its oxides, and its salts are almost unknown. On the other hand, certain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organotin_compound" title="Organotin compound">organotin compounds</a> are almost as toxic as <a href="/wiki/Cyanide" title="Cyanide">cyanide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-34">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Exposure to tin in the workplace can occur by inhalation, skin contact, and eye contact. The <a href="/wiki/Occupational_Safety_and_Health_Administration" title="Occupational Safety and Health Administration">Occupational Safety and Health Administration</a> (OSHA) has set the legal limit (<a href="/wiki/Permissible_exposure_limit" title="Permissible exposure limit">permissible exposure limit</a>) for tin exposure in the workplace as 2 mg/m<sup>3</sup> over an 8-hour workday. The <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Occupational_Safety_and_Health" title="National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health">National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health</a> (NIOSH) has determined a <a href="/wiki/Recommended_exposure_limit" title="Recommended exposure limit">recommended exposure limit</a> (REL) of 2 mg/m<sup>3</sup> over an 8-hour workday. At levels of 100 mg/m<sup>3</sup>, tin is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IDLH" title="IDLH">immediately dangerous to life and health</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This conversion is known as <i>tin disease</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Tin_pest" title="Tin pest">tin pest</a></i>. Tin pest was a particular problem in northern Europe in the 18th century as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organ_pipes" title="Organ pipes">organ pipes</a> made of tin alloy would sometimes be affected during long cold winters. There are anecdotal claims that tin pest destroyed some of Captain Scott's stores in the ill-fated expedition (see <a href="/wiki/Tin_pest" title="Tin pest">tin pest</a>). Some unverifiable sources also say that, during <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a>'s Russian campaign of 1812, the temperatures became so cold that the tin buttons on the soldiers' uniforms disintegrated over time, contributing to the defeat of the <a href="/wiki/Grande_Arm%C3%A9e" title="Grande Armée">Grande Armée</a>.<cite class="citation book">Le Coureur, Penny; Burreson, Jay (2004). <i>Napoleon's Buttons: 17 Molecules that Changed History</i>. New York: Penguin Group USA.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Napoleon%27s+Buttons%3A+17+Molecules+that+Changed+History&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pub=Penguin+Group+USA&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft.aulast=Le+Coureur&amp;rft.aufirst=Penny&amp;rft.au=Burreson%2C+Jay&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATin"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>, a persistent legend that probably has no background in real events. <cite class="citation book">Öhrström, Lars (2013). <i>The Last Alchemist in Paris</i>. Oxford: Oxford University Press. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-966109-1" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-966109-1">978-0-19-966109-1</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Last+Alchemist+in+Paris&amp;rft.place=Oxford&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2013&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-966109-1&amp;rft.aulast=%C3%96hrstr%C3%B6m&amp;rft.aufirst=Lars&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATin"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/> <cite class="citation journal">Cotton, Simon (2014). "Book review: The last alchemist in Paris". <i>Chemistry World</i>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Chemistry+World&amp;rft.atitle=Book+review%3A+The+last+alchemist+in+Paris&amp;rft.date=2014&amp;rft.aulast=Cotton&amp;rft.aufirst=Simon&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATin"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="external free" href="http://rsc.li/CW_140501" rel="nofollow">http://rsc.li/CW_140501</a></span>
